by consent of the prosecution, when it
will be set for hearing.
Mr. Aon and his party then crossed
back Into Judge Mogan's courtroom. Like
his Judicial brother over the way Judge
Mogan denied the motion of the defense
for a Sheriff jury in short order. In the
latter court Attorney Ach asked Chief
Lees if he was biased against the race
track either personally or in his official
capacity. Lees denied that he had the
least prejudice against the defendants and
said the course he is pursuing is prompted
only by duty and not by personal Inter
est. He did not personally superintend
the selection of jurors, but turned the
work over to different police captains, so
that the panel would come from differ
ent localities. The only instruction he
ever gave for such selection was to get
good men.
The cases will come up this morning as
in the other Police Court.
The Supervisors are still- receiving
words of commendation from all classes
of reputable citizens and organizations,
as the following communication will
show:
SAN FRANCISCO. March 16. 1899.
To the Supervisors of the City and County of
San Franciscoâ Honored Sirs: We, the officers
and' members of the Woman's Federation for
Public Good, do hereby extend to you our
heartfelt thanks for the stand you have taken
regarding horse racing. It has been a great
detriment to our city and we feel that your
action is deserving of the sincere gratitude of
all who are interested in the welfare of our
city and the safety of our homes. Again thank
ing you, I am, yours most respectfully,
_____ MAY KIMBALL,
Recording Secretary.
The police, under the direction of Chief
of Police Lees, are still most active and
vigilant in carrying out the provisions of
the new law. Patrolmen were stationed
yesterday on every street where there is
a poolroom, and a virtual blockade and
boycott will be maintained until the evil
is destroyed. It is a somewhat dangerous
undertaking now for downtown clerks to
patronize the poolrooms.
STAMPED WINE.
Barkeepers Must Affix the War
Stamp to Wine When They
/ -;'; Bottle. It. v]J;
; : By letter dated March 14, 1899, George
;W. : Wilson, Commissioner - of- - Internal
Revenue, notifies Collector John C. Lynch
cf the following important rulings:
1. If a customer buys from a wholesale
dealer a barrel of claret for lax use and the
dealer bottles It, he must stamp the bottles be
fore delivery. *
2. A customer buys a barrel of claret for' bar
use and the dealer delivers It to him. It is
a wine that cannot be drawn from the barrel
a bottle -at a time without stamping what la
left, and if bottled all at once it must be
securely corked. If he bottles this wine all at
one time, the bottles to be uncorked as occa
sion requires, and the wine sold over the bar
he must stamp the wine when bottled.
3. A customer buys a tank of wine for bar
use in order to obtain uniform wine and orders
it bottled by the dealer and has it sent to him
fifty or more bottles at a time, as' occasion
requires. The stamps must be affixed when the
wine is bottled.
4. A customer buys a tank of wine for bar
use. If he comes to the dealer's cellar and
bottles It himself, furnishing his own bottles,
he must stamp the wine as it is bottled.
The only case In which a battle filled from a
barrel Is not taxable Is where the bottle merely
serves the purpose of a decanter, being filled
from the barrel as occasion arises, the intent
to sell by the glass being confirmed by the
actual and constant use for that purpose.
In cases where the entire contents of a barrel
are transferred at one time into bottles, there
to be retained for some time, the allegation
that the wine Is ultimately to be sold by the
glass cannot be accepted, and the bottles ln
such cases must be stamped according to law.
Webster-btreet Improvements.
A meeting of the Western Addition Im
provement Club was held at Franklin
Hall last night. Dr. Charles V. Cross oc
cupied the chair. A report from the exec
utive committee stated that the Super
visors had taken notice of the club's pro
test against the bad condition of Bush
street. According to the opinion of tho
City and County Attorney, as filed, the
Sutter Street Railroad Company is re
sponsible for the repairing of the street.
A committee, consisting of Messrs. Sel
fridge. Henderson and Nobman, was ap
pointed to wait- on the Street Light Com
mittee of the Supervisors, with the view
of having electric lights placed on Web
ster street. Assembly bill 19, by Rlckard.
was condemned and a request forwarded
to Governor Gage to veto it.
Involuntary Bankruptcy.
Triest & Co., Friedman & Rogers and
other creditors of J. H. Jenkines of Valley
Ford, Sonoma County, yesterday peti
tioned the United States District Court
to declare Jenkines an insolvent. The
complaint alleges that he transferred his
property to M. 11. Le Barron and A. Kahn
creditors, with intent to prefer them to
the other creditors.

II m Market sweat. 8. F.-iS end X. _ Clr .___. _GREAT CAST IN "THE HUGUENOTS."
Gadski, De Lussan, Ma and - Ceppi Sing Meyerbeer's
Bombastic Masterpiece.-'
THE crowd that attended the
opera last night will go on record
as the largest of the Ellis season.
The police will" never permit an-
other like it. Every seat in the
large auditorium was occupied, and the
management only knows how many
admission tickets were sold. The aisles
and margins downstairs and in the
dress circle were blocked solid. No at
tempt was made to preserve the thin
nest sort of a passageway in the aisles
and doorways. The women could not
promenade, and he whose business or
pleasure encouraged going out between
acts was lucky to escape wit- a whole
skin â say nothing about the coat. The
Grand Opera House was simply
stretched. There was very" audible dip-"
order in the gallery during the early
part of the evening, but fortunately
nothing of a panicky nature happened.
The opera was Meyerbeer's bombas
tic masterpiece. "The Huguenots," with
a superlative cast: Gadski, the Valen
tine; Melba, the Marguerite; De Lus
ean, the page; Ceppi, the Raoul, and
Boudouresque the Marcel, and all the
subsidiary parts in good hands. The
choruses were strong and telling; the
orchestra under SeppilH was in the pink
of form. It would be hard to imagine
a better performance. But better and
more attractive operas there are by the
dozen, and th.' enthusiasm of the night
was hardly in proportion to the size of
the crowd. The cast was the magnet.
The crowd came because the three big
stars, Melba, Gadski and De Lussan,
were to sing. And it was a memorable
experience to hear these voices in one
production. It was especially satisfy
ing to hear Melba and Gadski on thÂ«
same night. Their .voices and methods
are so wonderful and unalike that the
special accomplishment of each singer
seemed enhanced. But even with the
finest vocalization and acting there are
long dull moments in "The Hugue
nots." Unquestionably this is the most
shapely and inspired of all Meyerbeer's
operas, but it weighs a ton just the
same, .so much of the music is so prosy
and pompous. It does not rush you
on to the sequel as Wagner does, as
even Verdi does in his later works; it
is brilliant, intense, dramatic at times,
but there are great broad perfunctory
deserts to cross that are very dry. It is
only a great cast that can make any
thing of "The Huguenots,." and even
then the stuffiness and gloom are not
entirely gilded over.
Mr. Ellis' cast was certainly mag
nificent; and the production throughout
was admirable, from, the mise-en-scene
to the ballet, which, while' almost ludi
crously small (eight and a premiere),
was alertly up to its business. The
concerted pieces and the tremendous
choruses were given out with zeal and
sonority, the masses of people 'were
well Btagemannered, and the principal
singers gave distinguished perform
ances. â¢". ""->''- â .â '*.-'- - : â :'
' Gadski's Valentine was another stun
ning tribute to this artist's remarkable
power and Versatility. It was a tenta
tive, poetic personation, beautifully
sung from that great sweeping vece of
hers, and beautifully proportioned in
interpretation. â¢ Not a false heroic
marred it. It swelled consistently till
the approach to the final tragedy, when
it bcame strenuously, passionately
dramatic. Ceppi played up to Gadski
in the right mood. He is one robust
tenor who realizes the importance of
repression, and his climaxes were legiti
mate and stirring. Not the least of
Ceppi's- amiabilities is his ability to
throw off a broad, ringing top note
without employing any of those man
nerisms that make tenors out to be
something less than men. . -â ,',;" ,^
Scalchi's .singing of the "page Will al
ways be remembered by this and the
laÂ«t generation of operagoers. De Lus
snn's was remarkable for its character.
chc played the boy to 'the life, with
charming foppery and swagger, and
.1-.. sang In a happy vein, Riving a
graceful and nrettily colored version
of the "Nc-biL Slgnors." Boudouresque's,
voice seemed not as lumlnant as usual,
but he presented a sturdy Marsello.
giving a certain, gruff emphasis to the
part that was quiTe in character.
In spite of her reported contact with
a piece of falling statuary. Melba was
dazzling and fit, and appeared to enjoy
herÂ«elf Immensely in the comparatively
small role of Marguerite. She wore her.
holiday diamonds and warbled as bird
lly as ever through her delicate duties
in the second act. It was just a morsel
of Melba at her most popular, and the
crowd thundered its gratitude.

a mighty fringe of gorgeous, perspiring

The management inaugurated a new
system and sold ''admissions," and there
were enough of "stand-up" seats sold to

In the gallery there was the mischief
to pay. The first three rows were by a
prudent few reserved at the rate of $2
per seat, but the regular habitue of the
top circle resented the â¢ "airs" of the
holders of numbered chairs and seated
themselves in "first come, first served"
fashion. A force of eighteen . policemen
vainly essayed -to assist ticket-holders to
the chairs their coupons called for, but
the will of the "unreserved" majority
prevailed, with the result that several
hundred dollars was returned to coupon
holders at the box-office.
The elect arrived earlier than usual and
forced their way in through a mob of ad
miring humanity that with delicious
franK passed upon the cut of a gown,
the set of a, coat, the arrangement of
jewels and even the personal beauty of
the representatives of the swell set. .
Mrs. Joseph Tobin. in dead white satin
and fleecy tulle, muffled from throat to
heels in a brocaded white opera coat, for
a moment rendered the mob speechless.
Then a pale-faced little girl in shirt waist
and sailor hat found . her breath and
gasped: "My Gawd, hut don't she look
awfully like" Tom rke's wife?" Lucky
Ton*: Fortunate Mrs. Tobin! Not every

she Id. One little girl in pink was told

proudly leaned was pointed out to the
â¢crowd f. : as "that guy that looks ready
made."
The new carriage system worked
superbly. and there were no annoying

appreciate and applaud. .'; Â-_

for taste, elegance and beauty; but there
were so many favorites with stanch sup
porters that the contest of necessity was
declared off.
Miss Helen Hopkins was without doubt
the most stunning girl in the auditorium,
and Miss Ethel Kceney. with her saucy.
little black patches showed up brighly
beside her in the Hopkins box.
Mrs. Will Crocker was remarkably
handsome. in a plain blue rhousseline so
altogether dainty and sweet and sia-.ple
that It must represent an appalling sum.
The foyer Was not so alive as on other
occasions, the "standing room" blocking
the aisles and making â - â¢> and entrance

McCarty. son of the famous "White

down the foyer the young man tramped,
now and then breathing deep and draw
ing his opera tile further over his nose
but never within earshot of the music. .
A pretty scene between acts was the
cafe. The ladies who braved the crowded
aisles assembled th'-re to discuss the mu
sic and the audience and the gowns. It
was a crowd of ladies bound for the re
freshment booth after the first act who

tected from the cold in a tan top coat,
bis official head ornamented by a black

soup stains on the assembled gorgeous

BUSINESS MEN ATTEND
THE NOONDAY SERVICE
THE first of the noonday services held at the Chamber of Commerce by the
Brotherhood of St. Andrew yesterday -was marked by the large congrega
tion of prominent business men. Bishop Moreland of Sacramento delivered
a sermon, hymns were sung, prayers repeated and benediction pronounced,
all in the short period of thirty minutes, after which the congregation dispersed
and returned to daily toil. The object of the meetings is to give an opportunity
to men occupied all the week and disinclined to attend church on Sundays of
attending divine service without much inconvenience to themselves.
The Episcopal clergy was well represented at yesterday's gathering. Hymn
books were â¢distributed by the ushers and an organ provided. The spacious cham
bers, .ordinarily devoted to the- consideration and disposition of worldly matters,
donned a sacred apearance. which was pronounced by the profusion of white
lilies arid the open Bible on the rostrum. After the opening hymn, the recital
of the creed. Bishop Moreland spoke in part as follows:
"I am addressing a congregation of business men. In the spirit of business I
will confine my remarks. I implore every one of you who cherishes a secret sin
to tear it from your heart. The man who conducts his business on lines of theft
and deception is too despicable to be called a thief. The man who places fraud
ulent wares on the market, sand in his sugar or adulterations on his shelves
is bereft of the Spirit of God and should reach for the hour of repentance at
once. Going to church will not save him, neither will it give him a title to
eternal life. He must amend his ways and tread the paths of righteousness, even
though Iris profits be less. .
"A business man's name on'the wrapper that binds his wares should he an
absolute guarantee of their purity and of his honesty and integrity. That men
may come to the realization of this I come to preach to you to-day. Oh, men.
do not think that because God does not strike you dead he will not pass judg
ment upon you. Death Is not a judgment. It is simply an introducer to a life
of paradise.. The judgment of God often visits men in the hours of their life.
"-.enounce evil before it is too late. Come back here to-morrow with a reso
lution to sin no more. In support of this, you will have the assistance of divine
power â a divine hand that sever- all chain*." ' *
The meetings will continue through the week. Among the clergymen who will
deliver sermons are: Rev. Herbert Parrish. Rev. Edgar J. Lion. Rev. T. J.
Lacey, Ph.D., and Right Rev. V,*. F. Nichols, D.D., Bishop of California. John
W. Wood, general secretary of the- New York society, will preach on "Keep
God's Commandments'^ at Wednesday's services.
PLAYS AND OPERA
AT THE THEATERS
De Koven's "Rcb Roy" Sue.
cessfnily Presented at
the Columbia.
A houseful of San Francisco's music
lovers greeted the Bostonians last night
in the Columbia Theater, where v.-; pro
duced for the first time in this city De
Koven's "Rob Roy." with Its kilted las
sies fair to look upon, its Scotch rebels
shouting for Prince Charlie, its sweet mel
odies and rol choruses, its scent of the
heather and Its pictured remembrances of
the days when the souls of true and loyal
men were sorely tried.
The company was favored with a house
of a quality that even Melba would not
scorn. Though the skies were darkened
with portents of rain the ladies came in
subdued epera toilets and their .escorts'
in evening dress.
"Rob Roy." despite some incongruous
patches of burlesque and Impossibility.
is a coherent work, in which the composer
of the score and the writer, of the book
seem to be at their best. Both parts that
go to make up the work have an out-of
door, breezy atmosphere. The kilted
Highlanders of Prince Charlie and the
red-coated soldiers of King George make
up the -personnel of the characters. The
music is De Kovenesque. There is a fine
effect of massed harmony in the choruses,
noticeably so in the military scenes., and
solos, duets and quintets are sprinkled
lavishly through the three acts of the
opera. "--- : , â 'Â¥â  '. â â â  : â .'â¢â  -
The first of the old favorites to receive
a hearty Californian greeting was Henry
Clay Barnabee, as Dugald MacWheeble,
Mayor of the town of Perth, and the old
time applause rang out to his song. "Take
Oft Your Hats to the Mayor and Make
Him a Low Salaam."

THE NEW MILITIA BILL.
BEFORE Governor Gage signs the bill for the
reorganization of the National Guard he should
call into his counsel some of the leading offi
cers of that organization. Not being a military man,
the Governor will naturally be unable to understand
some of the provisions of the measure in question,
but that he ought to understand them before he at
taches his approval is a proposition which can scarcely
be controverted. As originally framed the bill was
more objectionable than it is now, but it still contains
a large number of so-called reforms which are of
doubtful, utility, and which may,' instead of placing the
National Guard upon a more substantial footing, j
thoroughly disorganize it. : - - 1
The war with Spain left the militia of California in
a somewhat disheveled condition, consequent upon
the enlistment of a large number of its members in the
United States Volunteer service, each enlistment hav
ing in law operated to discharge a guardsman. - But
the disappearance of men and the resignation of offi
cers do not call for the changes of system which are
made by the bill now before the Governor. Under our
method of government the chief executive of State
and nation is the commander in chief of the army and
.navy. The reason of this is perfectly plain. In
Rome the military commanders used to be indepen
dent, and as a result they often utilized their soldiers
to place them upon the throne. It is to make the
military department of the Government subordinate
to the judicial, civil and political departments that all
the generals are appointed by the President or Gov
ernor and arc made subject to his command.
The reorganization bill under discussion erects the
major general of the California militia into an inde
pendent official. It cuts down the Governor's staff,
and requires that some of his militia appointments
shall be approved by the major general; it fixes the
term of the latter at a definite period, and authorizes
him to act independently of- the Governor in the dis- |
charge of many of his duties, notably in ordering en- I
campments and carrying them out.
This is all wrong in principle and should not be
tolerated. Depriving the Governor of power over the
National Guard will not tend to reorganize that insti
tution, while it will infract a very important feature of
our system of government. If Governor Gage will
call to his aid the prominent officers of the militia
they will show him how this bill is certain to operate
to the disadvantage of the organization. We. are sat
isfied from a cursory reading of the measure that it
increases the expense of maintaining the Guard. This
alone is an objection which ought to appeal forcibly
to the Governor, who is on record as an economist
of most uncompromising character.
There are some good things in the bill, but they are
counterbalanced by the attempt to make the major
general of the militia an irresponsible commander.
We think that such an innovation will generally meet
the disapproval of the National Guardsmen.
The court which gave Dick Williams a new trial,
and then another, and promised him a third, was not
the Supreme Court of the State, as the entire matter
was within Federal jurisdiction. Inadvertently a
writer on this paper had accepted the theory that the
fault of William's escape lay with the Supreme Court.
An ex-convict emerging from an immurement of
ten years finds that the gold he buried before incar
ceration has been dug up! We do not hesitate to de
nounce this as not .having been a square deal. If there
is honor among thieves, now is the time for some
body to come forward.
Filipinos must not burn any of the American pris
oners who fall into their hands. . This country .seems
â¢ rot to be averse to war. out to be satisfactory it has
to be conducted on at least a semi-civilized plan.
A Seattle incendiary has burned a church. If it is
any satisfaction for him to know it,' he is the meanest
creature. of his kind, and the best of the outfit to es
cape lynching got more than he deserved.
The Deputy Sheriff who murdered a boy at Colma
seems to have the worst excuse ever advanced. At
least, if anybody ever had a worse one he has been
hanged before now.
Senator Perkins says that'he kept out of the Sena-
t orial contest. 'He did this from choice. There were
several others who were never really in it, but who
tried to be.

THE CALL fIND PUBLIC OPINION
THE most noted contest in the history of Ameri
can politics closed when the Legislature ad
journed. ...â '. T
â¢ There had been failures before to elect a Senator.
There had been contests .which ended in taking up
side candidates and electing them, by the help of the
minority, like Christiancy in Michigan, Cameron in
Wisconsin and Tipton in Nebraska. But in each case
the. scat was contested for by the incumbent. Zach
"Chandler in Michigan, Mat Carpenter in Wisconsin
and Thayer in Nebraska were pushed aside by the fu
sion of a Republican minority with the Democratic
minority. .â¢'
. Here the case was different. A change in party con
trol had found a seat vacated by a Democrat to be
filled by a Republican. ' The record of the outgoing
Senator, with -but- little exception, was strong and
commendable, and his great ability had proved itself
in the Senate. His Republican colleague, without' the
advantage of a legal training, is yet so equipped in the
school of experience that he ranks well up and is a
most useful and creditable Senator.
It was the aspiration of the Republican party to fill
the vacant seat with a Senator worthy in ability to suc
ceed White and the equal in influence and usefulness
to Perkins.
When the candidacy of Burns appeared the party
was appalled. He represented a force that had here r
tofore been invincible. For more than thirty years the
Southern Pacific Railroad had never lost a battle in
California politics. No matter which party had a
majority in the State, the wishes of that corporation
had never been resisted nor denied successfully by
either. Indeed, so supreme had its power become that
it did not have to solicit an alliance. Both parties
sought its favor, and the one that was denied, seldom
put up a stout fight, but was content to' wait until it
was needed, when the fickle corporation would change
and use it as the instrumentality of its purpose. With
this record behind the power that supported him
Burns had grounds for the swagger with which he en
tered the fight. The Call recognized the natural
politics involved in giving the Senator to Southern
California, but we had no candidate. For reasons that
are now history we opposed both Grant and Burns.
As the long fight wore on it became plain that the
heretofore irresistible force behind Burns would lose
iio point nor spare any resource to effect his election,
and after clearing the air in the revelations of Speaker
Wright's serpentine and sinister part, thereby laying
bare also the ante-election incidents in Grant's cam
paign, we enlisted all the resources of The Call in the
service of the majority opinion and public judgment
of the State, which clearly ran against the election of
Burns. ..... !
The contest settled down finally to a siege. The
majority of the Republican members, representing a
majority of the party and the people, and standing
for the State's good name, stood the siege well. They
pursued no offensive tactics, but stood firmly on the
defense. The excellence of their discipline and their
loyalty to the right were proved by the fact that from
the .first ballot to the last only six men deserted them.
Every blandishment which can dazzle men and make
them forget tlie future and the past in the delirium
of the moment was exhausted, but the Republican
Old Guard stood firm, yielding not. We believed that
cur position was right when The Call announced it,
and our initiative was taken in advance of the use of
means for ascertaining public opinion, . but we as
sumed that the people were right on this issue, and
our confidence was not misplaced. The Call was in
line with the party and the people, and therefore won
its fight. It voiced public opinion when it pulled
Wright out of the Speaker's chair, in which he had
sat only seventeen days, enjoying briefly the fruits of
chicane and deception, and The Call has continued to
utter the public will to the close of this battle royal,
from which Burns retires defeated and discredited,
and in which the Southern Pacific Railroad has met
the first defeat in its history.
We by no means underestimate the value of the for
titude shown by Grant, Barnes and Scott. Grant' de
ported himself with dignity and decency, self-restraint
and wisdom, while Barnes and Scott never lost sight
of the fact that they, with Grant, were less candidates
for the Senate than they were trustees of the party's
future and the State's honor. Through it all The Call
has fought at the front, has fought fairly and has won
every objective point which it sought. The Southern
Pacific has discovered that ft is not the sole power in
this State, for the people of the State, must be
reckoned with. We* have not set down anything in
malice against that corporation. It has rights which
we will as readily defend against demagogues and
anarchists as we have just defended the people against
the corporation in a matter where it had no rights at
stake and wherein its presence should have been -un
known. We have taught the lesson that a newspaper
to have influence and power must align itself with en
lightened public opinion, and then must fight without
flinching. *
DIPLOMACY IN THE PHILIPPINES.
WHILE the results of the fighting between our
troops and the Filipinos have been on the
whole satisfactory, it is clear that an- attempt to
establish peace throughout the Philippines by strictly
military methods would entail a long and costly strug
gle. We could easily dispose of the insurgents if they
would come forth as one host in battle array, like the
Dervishes at Omdurman, but it will not be so easy
to track a thousand separate bands of marauders
through the jungles and the mountains.
From the reports that come to us from Manila it
appears the Filipinos have had sense enough to learn
already the difference between an American and a
Spanish force. In the earlier fights the insurgents
stood their ground with some firmness, and in one or
two instances even took the offensive and attacked our
lines with no other arms than bows and arrows.
They have now abandoned tactics of that kind and
are fighting like fleas on the jump. They snap at us
in one place, and when we reach for them they are
somewhere else. . -Â,-.
As a consequence of these tactics on the part of
the insurgents our troops have been compelled to re
sort to what are known as "punitive expeditions"-
that is to say, detachments are sept out to destroy.
property^ when they cannot find the enemy. Thus we
are told that a recent expedition, after having scat
tered a band of Filipinos, proceeded to burn five. miles
of huts and to destroy all the rice in the storehouses
and the growing crops in the fields. -â ""'-"â¢
Punitive tactics are very effective in civilized coun
tries and in the temperate zone, but are considerably
less so among semi-barbarians, in the tropics. The
Filipino is not much dismayed by the destruction of
his bamboo hut, for he can build another without any
cost for raw material and with no labor except that
of his wife and children. Neither does he care much
for his fields in a land where nature affords an abun
dant supply of vegetable food, and where, owing to
the climate, the amount of food required to sustain
life is very little. - .
For these reasons it is probable the settlement of
the Philippine imbroglio will depend more upon the
THE SAN FRANCISCO CAT.I.; TUESDAY, 31 ARCH 21, 1899.
commissioners sent out by the President than upon
the army. It is noted that the natives have been more
impressed by the kind treatment accorded to prisoners
taken in battle than by the valor of our troops or the
deadliness of our arms. -When.it is clearly impressed
upon them that we do not mean to reduce them- to
such a condition of wretchedness as \hat imposed by
Spanish rule they will probably be willing to listen to
reason and accept our government peacefully as long
as we remain in the islands.
In the meantime it is certain" that in our dealings
with a people whom Kipling has described as "half
savage and half child" we shall have tq humor them a
good deal. Punishment for any attack upon our lines
must of course be swift and sharp. It will never do
to let them get the impression' in their minds that we
cannot whip them. At the same time it is going to be
so hard to catch the villains in order to whip them
that a considerable amount of coaxing will be judi
cious in the interests of economy.
UPON no set of our State officials is there rest
ing at this time. a more serious responsibility
than that with which -the Regents of the Uni
versity are charged by the duty of selecting a suc
cessor to President Kellogg. It is hardly too much to
j say that the. whole work of higher education in Cali
i fornia will be more or less affected by the personal in
! fluence of the man chosen, and therefore the choice to
j be made is one of no-ordinary moment.
â¢ Universities, ever since the days of the academy
' founded by Plato, have performed an important ser
vice in shaping the course of civilization, but never in
human history was the influence of such institutions
so potent as at this time. The increased reliance of
men upon science to devise solutions for the social
.problems and needs of the age has given the uni
versity a leadership which it never had before, and
has rendered the personal characters of its president
and its professors matters of no small concern to the
community. â¢ â¢
The time when a university was simply a seat of
I scholarship is past. The people now turn to the uni
] versities for lecturers on all forms of knowledge and
!on all issues that arise. From farmers' clubs, from j
! literary societies, from university extension associa- i
; tions, from reform organizations, and even from politi-'
i cal committees, come demands upon them for a j
| scientific study of the problems of the time and a |
â  popular exposition of them.
The presidency of a university is now a vantage
point from which a strong, clear-headed, wise man
can make himself widely useful to the community. A
small man in such a position is worse than nothing,
for his inability to use-*the power the position gives
renders that power valueless to the community, and
one of the greatest forces of civilization is therefore
lost to that people.
Regent Rodgers, who has just returned from an j
extensive tour made for the purpose of seeking out j
a man well fitted for the presidency of the University, |
is reported to have stated that he has investigated the '
I qualifications of ten. men, and is prepared to submit
; a report at the meeting of the Regents in April. This i
probably means that the question will then be settled
and the selection made. That being so, it is well at j
j this time to remind the Regents that they are choos- j
: ing- a man not merely to teach young men and ;
women the learning that can be found in books, but
! a leader of the higher thought of the State. If they
i cannot find at this time a man who measures up to
j that standard they had better postpone the election
! and put the presidency in commission for a while.
AUSTRALIAN BUSINESS METHODS
A RECENT number of the Exporter, a Sydney
publication devoted to the export trade of the
Australian colonies, gives, concerning the fruit
trade with Europe, a good deal of information likely
to be not only interesting but instructive to Califor
nia!*?, since it shows the vigor with which the people
of those far-off countries are striving to compete with
us in that line of industry.
From the reports given it appears the Australians
ship to England not only fresh fruit but even fresh
vegetables, and the announcement is made that aspar
agus and green peas arriving in England between No
vember and March are sure to bring good prices. The
chief difficulty to be overcome in the trade in fresh
fruit is that of preserving it during the Jong voyage
across the ocean, though of course the heavy freight
rates also constitute a handicap on the enterprise.
Both in Victoria and in New South Wales the fruit
men, aided by their Governments, arc energetically
ard scientifically striving to master these obstacles.
It i** noted by the Exporter that the Victorian Gov
ernment grants a bonus on fruit exports, and in that.
way/offsets' the freight charges and stimulates pro
ducer, and shippers to devise some means of pre
serving the fresh fruit so as to make the shipments
profitable . â¢
' Several plans have been tried, but none seems to be
wholly satisfactory. The one most in favor at present
â¢seems .to be what is known as the "Southerland pro
cess," which amounts virtually to inclosing the fruit
in waxed paper bags.. In the meantime the Govern
ment has been lending its aid to the work and the
Agricultural Department is experimenting â¢ with" a
process of preserving fresh fruit by means of ster
ilized air. It is said that some apricots treated in
that way by the department were kept in good con
dition for forty-three days, but the bloom had faded
and the fruit would not be attractive in the market.
It is worth noting that the British correspondent
of the Exporter advises fruit and vegetable shippers
to send their goods direct to Manchester instead of
London or Liverpool, on the ground that the markets
of the two last-named cities are the general dumping
ground for the exports of all nations, while Man
chester, with. the swarming populations of the manu
facturing cities around it, is a comparatively over
looked market for such articles.
Another illustration of the comprehensive manner
with* which the Australians are seeking- to increase
the consumption of the products of their industry is
found in an announcement in the same journal that
the Government of Victoria has induced the railroads
to serve a tumbler of colonial wine with lunch or
dinner at all their eating-houses without extra charge
and to furnish it by the bottle at a shilling a quart.
By that means it is expected to make the use of native
wine common among the people and thus build up a
large home market for it. â¢ x
It will be seen from these reports, all of. which are
given in the ordinary course of a trade review, that
the Australians, though far from the great markets of
the world, are not sitting in idleness waiting for some
one to come along and help them. Through their
Governments as well as by individual enterprise they
are working out a solution of their difficulties and
bid fair to succeed.
â  There was something distressing in the thought of
the electrocution of a woman in New York, but now
that it is over it seems to have been the proper course.
With the Oregon- at Manila some of the powers
would better, put on their good behavior.

fore we enter this room."
Perraultâ l did not intend to ("light the

When it came up for the -second time

The report, which follows, is interesting;
showing as it does that the city pays one

PAN FRANCISCO,' March 20. 1899;
To the Honorable the- Board of Supervisors
of the City and County of San FranciscoâGen
tlemen: Your Committee on Finance and Au
diting beg leave to report in the matter of the
bills of the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company and. the Pacific Gas Improvement
Company for the months of December ' and
January, as follows:
The Superior Court has decided that the con
tracts under which gas and electricity were
supplied to the city and county of San Fran
cisco are void, but that the companies are en
titled to reasonable compensation for com
modities- furnished, and that it is the duty of
the board to fix such reasonable compensa
tion. Your committee has held an investiga
tion, .giving full arid fair opportunity to all
parties in Interest to be heard, and herewith
submits a sfenographlc report of such Investi
gation for the information of the board. We
have, elicited the following facts, as an ex
amination of said report will show:
That the San Francisco Gas and Electric
Company Is a combination of the San Fran
cisco Gaslight and the Edison Light and Power
Company: that before or after such combina
tion mfcst of the competing companies that had
been organized for the purpose of supplying the
city with lighting (gas and electricity) have
been absorbed and that the present capital
stock upon which dividends are paid does not
represent their real value; that rates are kept
excessively high in order to pay dividends on
fictitious capitalization;- that in the matter of
electric light the city pays more. in many in
stances, than is charged to private consumers:
that the cost of gas delivered to the consumer
ls less than $1 a thousand cubic feet: that
competing companies will. if given facilities,
supply the city with electric light of equal can
dle power for at least 33H per cent less than
is now charged the city. For these reasons we
Firstâ That the electric light bills In dispute
be settled on a basis of 25 per cent less than
the amount charged and that the gas bills be
settled at the rate of $1 35 per thousand cubic
feet.
Secondâ That the rate to consumers be fixed
on a graduated scale, as follows: Beginning
July 1, 1889, one year. $1 50 per thousand-, and
for the years succeeding that the rate be fixed
â¢respectively at $1 45. $1 35, $1 30 and $1 25. thus
at the end of live years reducing the price of
gas to $1 25 per thousand cubic feet to con
sumers; that these prices will yield a fair and
reasonable return on the real value of . the
company's plant, used for. the manufacture and
distribution Of gas ani electric light.
Third We recommend that new specifications
be prepared for lighting the city streets,
squares, public buildings, with gas and elec
tric light, which will admit competition. -and
that new proposals bo Invited and ln accord
ance with the Judgment of the court a new con
tract let. "' : ' : â â ;

DR. WEBB AND THE
NICKEL-IN-THE-SLOT
CAPTURED BY ITS SEDUCTIVE
- INFLUENCES.
He Struck a Royal Plush and Now
Has a Machine of His
Own.
The Vanderbilts have stamped the nick
! el-in-the-slot machine as the proper thing.
( The deceptive little box with its straights
! and flushes and full hands, which sepa
| rate so many nickels from their specula- '
' tive owners, has won the hearts of the |
! Vanderbilts. and that is almost enough to j
j wipe out all the harsh things the preach- I
ers and Supervisors have said about them.
The seductive jingle of the nickel falling i
into the box, the click, click of the op
erating rod and the whirr, whirr of the :
cards have literally entranced the Van- !
derbilts, and now the Vanderbilts have
i one of their own.
Nickel-in-the-slot gambling was the first !
! purse-loosening amusement that caught
; the eyes of the Vanderbilt party
i when they rolled into the State in their
I private pleasure cars. At Santa Barbara
; Dr. W. S. Webb, brother-in-law to Come;
-; lius Vanderbilt Jr. and president of the
! Wagner Car Company, dropped into a
; cigar stand on the main street and no
, ticing, the nickel-in-the-slot machine
! standing invitingly on the counter he in
quired what its use was. The cigar dealer
i explained to his inquisitor that all it cost
I to play it was a nickel, and a person was
! liable at any time to get a royal flush.
"If you get a royal flush you get a hun
i dred fine cigars," said the cigar merchant,
1 with the air Of a man who was used to
paying off on a royal flush two or three
times every day.
"A hundred for a royal flush, and it only |
costs* a nickel to play it," . retorted the l
doctor. "Well, I guess I'll try it."
"The main guy of the Vanderbilt par*- |
ty," as Patsy Carroll would term Dr. !
Webb if. he were telling the story, threw a
half dollar on the counter and asked for j
i a few nickels.
The cigar dealer, only â¢ too willing to
change ' half dollars when there was a
prospect of somebody going up against his
own game, handed the doctor five shining !
nickels and -â¢ quarter, winking at the j
same time to some of his statues standing
in front.
The man of money and cars - dropped I
.the first nickel in the slot. Click went the i
rod and then the. cards began to turn. j
When they stopped Dr. Webb began to
count his hand. .
'Ten, jack, queen, king, ace, and all one
coâ" .'â¢*'â¢
"All one what?" chipped in the cigar
dealer, as he stretched his neck to see Dr.
Webb's hand.
"All one color. That's a royal, flush,
isn't it?" asked the doctor, with. an as
sumed air of innocent surprise.
"I think that's right," said the crest
fallen dealer. ' â  â¢
. Dr. Webb got his cigars, but he liked
; the game and remained at the stand some
i little time playing the machine.
When the Vanderbilts arrived at the
i foot of Townsend street the other day Dr.
| Webb came up town and invested $20 in
i a brand new nickel-in-the-slot machine,
I and he didn't say it was for the baby,
' either.
Evidently the doctor thinks there is
more profit in runing a nickel-in-the-slot
i machine than building cars. Or perhaps
the doctor intends to keep dropping nick
els in the machine until he gets another .
royal flush and pay himself. for the win
ning. Or he may want to play the ma
chine a while and see how much percent
age he would lose if he continued to go

CALIEORNIANS IN NEW YORK.
NEW YORK, March 20.â A. J. Hanford
of San Francisco is at the Marlborough.
THE FIGHT
FOR DECENCY
SAN : JOSE, Cal, March 20, 1899. .
Editor of the San Francisco Call: I
congratulate The Call and Chronicle for
their successful fight for clean politics in
the selection of a United. States Senator
and preventing the election of Boss Burns.
As much as we regret not being repre
sented in the Senate by a man, that would
command our respect and capable of
serving the people with distinguished
ability, the better class of the Republican
party will thank you for your firm stand
against the boss, which has saved the
State from disgrace had he been 'elected.
I know I voice the sentiments of a ma
jority of the Republican party in Santa
Clara County when I so express myself.

AMERICAN MAIL
THROWN ASIDE
BY CANADIANS
Twenty Tons Rotting
Near Skaguay.
CARRIERS WILL NOT TOUCH IT
â ,
: THE INTERNATIONAL POSTAL
AGREEMENT VIOLATED.
_â
Only Letters Bearing the Dominion
. y . Postage Axe at the Present
Time Transported Over the- â¢
Pass by Contractors.
The postal service to and from the
Klondike has been most unsatisfactory
for many months, and friends and rela
tives of the gold seekers in the frozen
j north have long been puzzled over the
fact that their letters have never reaphed
their destination. The fault lies with the
Canadian contractors who are employed
to transport the mail. Letters come out
from Dawson and other points in fairly
good condition and as quickly as can be
reasonably expected. But the mail going
the other way is sidetracked.
The explanation of this condition is the
fact that the contractors who bargained
with the Canadian Government to carry
the postal matter from Skaguay to Daw
son refuse to handle any mall which does
not bear the Canadian postage. Mail
matter from the United States is thrown
aside when it leaves the steamer, and at
the present time it is estimated that
twenty tons bearing American stamps is
piled up in heaps at Skaguay. Thousands
and thousands of letters from anxious
friends and relatives who have not heard
from the venturesome men who went Into
the land of ice to dig for gold are rotting
in the open or being blown into the gul
lies and sea and will never be delivered.
The anxiety of the snow-imprisoned
miners can be imagined when month after
month passes and no word comes from
home. Then, too. there are hundreds of
unfortunates who staked all their ready
I cash to defray the expenses of the inward
I trip, and this money has been exhausted
since they reached the alluring gold fields.
These men have been unsuccessful, and
they are hanging around Dawson waiting
for money from home to bring them back
to civilization. The letters containing re
mittances are in the discarded mail at
the seaboard, and the persons to whom
they are addressed are in desperate
straits in .consequence thereof.
Frank Jaqua, a young man from Pater
son, N. J., who went- over the Chukoot a
year ago, has returned by the same
ink Jaqua, a young man from Pater-
X. J., who went over the Chilkoot a
ago, has just returned by the same
route. He brought messages to several
people in this city from stranded miners
to whom money had been sent, but they
had never received it and could not un
derstand why their correspondents here
had not responded to their requests.
Jaqua made a personal Investigation of
affairs at Skaguay, and he solemnly de
clares that carload upon carload of mail
from the United States is lying. there in
heaps and that the Canadian carriers will
not transport it over the pass. He in
formed a Call- reporter that the only sure
means of getting a. letter into Dawson is
| to first send it over the Canadian line, to
j Victoria or some other point, enclosed in
another envelope and have some friendly
person repost -it with- a' Canadian stamp
I attached. 'â¢ â¢ * " * "
. Since his return to civilization Jaqua
has received information from his home
.that his relatives and friends, wrote 'â  him
repeatedly and that they were surprised
to read in his letter from the Klondike
that he was not receiving any mail. He
located the cause at Skaguay and called
on the Canadian postal authorities there,
but got no satisfaction. He was curtly
informed by the contractors that they
were not paid to carry ' American mail
over the passes and that they would not
touch the post coming from the States.
A well known lawyer in this city whose
brother- is mining near Dawson, when he
learned from the Paterson man the true
condition of affairs; decided to report the
matter to the postal authorities at Wash
ington. The refusal of the Canadian car
riers to handle American mail is a viola
tion of the international postal agreement
and in all probability the Dominion Gov
ernment will be requested to investigate.
In the meantime all persons wishing to
communicate with friends in the Klondike
will be wise to send their missives to some
one in Canada and have them remail
them. â¢ â  .
Jaqua does not think the Klondike a
very good place for miners. His partner
is an expert mining engineer and both say
the country has been overrated. They
went there as the representatives of a
wealthy syndicate, in" which Vice Presi
dent Hobart is interested, and during the
past year have traveled extensively
through the regions where digging is go
ing on. While a few lucky argonauts have
struck it rich, the majority of the thou
sands who rushed into Alaska expecting
to get wealth quickly will return with
empty pockets. â¢
City Seeks Lands.
Suit was riled yesterday by the city and
county against- the executors of the will
of the late James G. Fair to quiet title
to a piece of land running from Bay
street, from the corner of Fillmore street,
to the bay- This piece of land is divided
into what is called "water lots" by the
Fair estate, but it seems that the city and
county questions the title of the estate
thereto and will fight for their possession.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Silver weaker.
New York exchange higher.
Wheat and Barley lower.
-Oats easier. Corn end Rye dull.
Hay. Bran and Middlings declined.
No change in Flour.
Beans undisturbed.
Potatoes strongly held.
Onions rather firmer.
Liberal receipts of Vegetables.
Butter lower again. Eggs quiet and steady.
Three cars of Eastern Poultry arrived.
Oranges in larger receipt and weaker,
Another decline in Hogs.
Coffee active.
FRUIT AND SALMON IN ENGLAND.
The London Grocers' Gazette of March 4 re
ports as follows: Fruitsâ supplies of Cali
fornlan fruits are going steadily into consump
tion; the general demand ls good and a further
advance of <*dQls per case is being asked. At
Liverpool he Marie Hackfeld has arrived with
3e 275 cases. Salmonâ Supplies at Liverpool
have been augmented by the arrival of 29,921
cases of Alaska by the Marie .Hackfeld and
88,815 cases I raser River salmon per the Aca
mas. The market continues somewhat dull,
but values are practically unchanged with the
exception that in some cases the recent advance
of t.d on Alaska has been partially lost. The
consumptive demand is not quite so active as
had been anticipated, .but an early improve
ment is looked tor.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The storm noted in Washington this morn
ing has moved southeastward and is now cen
tral in Wyoming. A second disturbance has
moved in from the ocean during the day and
is central north of Washington.
The pressure has risen over the Pacific Slope
during the past twelve hours.
Rain or snow has fallen over the Pacific
Slope from the British possessions to Mexico,
.with he exception of Southern Arizona.
The temperature has fallen over the northern
portion of the country, the greatest fall being
in Montana.
Forecast made at San â  Francisco for thirty
hours tiding midnight, March 21. 1599:
Northern Californiaâ Cloudy, with ' showers
Tuesday:, cooler: light variable winds.
Southern California â Cloudy, with showers
in the mountains Tuesday; fresh west wind. *
Nevadaâ Tuesday: colder.
Utahâ Snow Tuesday; colder.
Arizonaâ Cloudy, with snow in north portion
Tuesday.
Sir. Francisco and vicinityâ Cloudy and
threatening, with showers Tuesday; light var
iable winds.
G. H. WILLSON. Local Forecast Official.
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. March 20.â market, after
a sensational movement in sugar and the local
transportation shares, closed weak with mixed
r.et changes. Interest centered largely in sugar,
in which the transactions were over 191,000
shares, with a total of about 900,000 shares.
Sugar opened with an advance of about 8
points, ran off 3% and then touched 182, with
a subsequent plunge of IS points, closing with
a net loss of about S points. . The extraordinary
movement of this stock had a wide influence
in other directions; Brooklyn Transit was
lifted over 6 points and then lost much of the
gain. Pronounced strength appeared in the
Vanderbilts. Union Pacifies, Northwestern, the
coalers and Chicago Terminal, while some of
the internationals made a show of strength in
the early dealings in sympathy with the higher
range in London. . The tobacco stocks were
extremely feverish. American Tobacco broke
6%, rallied '.< and left off with a loss of 2; Con
tinental Tobacco slumped 14*4 and was off 3
points at the close. The liquidation in these
stocks was attributable to the reported forma
tion of a new opposition tobacco company.
Manhattan retained the greater part of a 4
point rise. There was a disposition of the
professional element to let- commission house
purchasers have stocks, and subsequently
while sugar was tumbling and call money
reached 9 per cent, prices were freely offered
down. Standard stocks, while easing with the
recent market, receded in an orderly manner,
and the reaction in those securities from the
best did not exceed two points except In special
cases. Money on call toward the close eased
with the last loan at 4 and offered at 3, but
the market failed to respond to the changed
conditions due to the supply of money offered
and the reaction ' was unchecked to the end.
The strength in Brooklyn Transit was coinci
dent with the published interview with the
Brooklyn Rapid Transit Railroad to the effect
that an exchange of the majority of Brooklyn
Transit elevated stocks for Brooklyn Rapid
Transit stock ha been arranged. The ad
vance in Union Pacific and New York Central
was timed with the announcement of the re
turn of the leading interest In Vanderbilt
stocks, an 1 gave rise to a renewal of rumors
that Union Pacific might be absorbed by the
New York Central. The opinion -was ex
pressed because of the action of the market
that the rise in London, which was a pur
chaser at the opening only, was manipulated
from this side to facilitate the liquidation
which was evident in the market."
Bond prices to-day were raised generally.
considerable activity developing in the St.
Louis Southwestern issues, Texas Pacific sec
onds and Wisconsin Central firsts. Total sales.
$3,430,000. United States 3s and the hew 4s ad
vanced 'Â« and the old 4s % In the bfd price.

shares. Including: Atchison preferred 23,220,

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK,. March 20.â The Commercial
Advertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were excessively idle to-day
awaiting the Easter holidays from Friday to
Tuesday. Americans followed New York slav
ishly. There was slight professional buying
early, particularly of Union Pacific, on a revi
val of the Vanderbilt consolidation reports.
New . York bought, then sold, and the close ,
was heavy except for Union Paoiflc.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, March 20.â There were a number of
strong factors In the wheat market at the corn-
'. mencement, and traders started business in a !
j bullish mood. Cables were higher and freezing i
I weather was reported in .the wheat belt, which
i It was thought might prove damaging to the I
| growing plant. The statistics were also re- j
| garded as rather favorable to the buying side. j
World's shipments were considerably smaller j
than expected, and the amount :on ' passage '
showed an increase for the week of only 80,000
bushels. May opened %i&%c higher at 67%@
68% c, and as crop damage claims became more I
numerous than ever the price rose to .6S , .â¢
68% c. Apparently reliable reports from both
the Northwest and Southwest regarding farm- :
ers' deliveries were to the effect that receipts
.were being heavily reduced and in many cases j
were insufficient for the requirements of the j
local millers. As the senders of this informa- â¢
tion backed up their claims with messages to I
buy, shorts became nervous and covered freely,
advancing May to 68% c. The market ruled very !
firm the greater part of the session, but near j
the end the taking of profits by scalper's re- I
suited in a reaction to 67% c, and the price at \
the close was 67% c buyers.
Light country acceptances and small receipts i
strengthened corn. A fairly good speculative i
business was done at 'some 'improvement
In prices. A good cash demand ana consid- '
erable buying of July by the country helped I
oats. â¢As the session neared the' close, how- j
ever, selling caused a loss of more than half j
the advance. May began %c up, at 26c, rose to |
26% c and closed at 25% c.
Provisions opened strong In sympathy with |
the buoyancy of the grain markets, but later j
lost the advance on selling out by the tired I
longs,' who â  were evidently awaiting firmness i
on which to market their holdings. May pork
closed 5@7%c lower, lard 2%@6c lower and ribs I
unchanged.

LONDON WOOL SALES. '
LONDON, March 20.â final week of the
present series of the wool auction sales opened
with a large attendance of buyers. The offer
ings numbered 9633 bales and were chiefly of
good medium' stock and a fair' portion of In
ferior. There was good competition and better
scoured merinos were in great request. Ger
man and French buyers operated in low cross
breeds, although honie buyers secured the bulk.
Greasy merinos were equally divided.

COFFEEâ C. E.-. Bickford's circular gives the
receipts at this port for the first two months
of the year at 31,871 bags, against 24,039 bags
during the same time in 1898. The sales from
first- hands were 21,352 bags, against 18,580. The
stock In first hands March 1 was 16,357 bags,
against 10,701. The world's visible supply
March 1 was 6,522,000 -bags, against 6.024, 925.
The circular says:
"Our last circular was dated February 7,
since which time the market has been active
and firm for the very highest and the very
lowest qualities, and decidedly irregular and
soft for all intermediate grades. Having but
one small arrival since the 19th of February,
the market has not afforded a sufficient- supply
of those qualities most in request to satisfy
the demand and a large' business Is still pos
sible in strictly prime to fancy Guatemala at
the top prices given, and also in inferior coffees
of any growth costing not over 7%c per pound.
In addition to the sales given at. the end of
this circular, quite a- business has been done
in unwashed Salvadors for forward deliveries
at slightly less than the basis of present spot
quotations, and Is still going on.
"The New York market for Brazil coffees has
no features of encouragement to holders, the
quotations there for spot and futures having
steadily gone off. ' â  >'
"To-day' b first hand stock consists of 1832
bags Costa Rica, Nicaragua. . 6487 Salvador.
7663 Guatemala and 16.1 Mexican: in all 16,445'
bags, as against 21,304 bags same time last
year." - .'â 'â -. ' â¢ , . . -

THi. b'iOGK MARKET.
Rather more tone was shown by mining
stocks owing to the publication of the weekly
reports showing considerable .activity at the
mines, and some stocks sold a few cents higher
than 'on Saturday. A telegram from the pump
read as follows: "Machinery for rearranging
the elevator of 'the Rlsdon pumping plant ar
rived here on .Saturday night. It is now being
put together and will. be in place in a few days
arid pumping resumed."
Local securities were fairly active, with a
rise in Hana Plantation.
The- Belcher assessment of 10c per share will
be delinquent in the company's office to-day.
The Gwin Mining Company of California
paid a dividend of 25c per share on March 10.
- Th- Best & Belcher and Gould & Curry com
panies have repurchased the steam hoist plant
now in place at the Obiston shaft from the
Fulton foundry, to whom It had been sold long
ago, and will begin the erection of a new shaft
house and gallows frame for the same.
In the Sierra Nevada mine the north drift
from the northwest drift on the 140 level was
advanced 22 feet; total length 233 feet. The
face Is in low-grade quartz. Since the weekly
letter was written an upraise has been started
from the end of this drift. .In the Union shaft
workings of the Sierra Nevada the south drift
from the top of the raise, SO feet above the
800 level, is out 112 feet. The face Is In por
phyry and clay. .___._-
In the Best & Belcher and Gould & Curry
mines, ln the drift connecting with the Obis
ton shaft, a great mass of old timbers and rock
has been cleaned out from and near the' shaft.
A little air is beginning to' circulate through
the shaft. â¢ - 'ââ  * ,_._..,
In the Hale & Norcross mine the work of
cleaning out- and repairing the connecting drift
between the main incline and the Sutro tunnel â 
south lateral branch is * progressing favorably.
The official report from the Consolidated Cal
ifornia and Virginia mine for the past week
states that on the 1400-foot level, at the point
of. intersection of the west drift with the north
and southwest drifts, there is some heavy
ground that has to be carefully timbered, and
five sets, or thfrty feet, have been repaired.
A line of air pipe has been carried fro*- the
shaft to the face of the drift and connected
with a tour-Toot fan and a five-horse-power
electric motor that will be started up as soon
as the water is again turned into the pressure
pipe. It has been decided. to run directly west
to the Consolidated Virginia shaft from the
Junction of the northwest and southwest drifts
on this level instead of following the north
west drift, as the distance is shorter to reach
the stopes and there is quite a stretch of un
prospected ground between this point and the
hanging wall of the old bonanza. 1650 levelâ
The drift ha been cleaned out to the point of
Intersection with the footwall raise, used when
prospecting work was being done at the time
when all work was suspended in July last.
Large quantities of eleven and fifteen-inch air
pipe have been taken out of caving and aban
doned drifts, and there is still a lot of T rail
that can be profitably removed from this level.
Air passages and main drift will have to be
kept open on this level for the purpose of se
curing a circulation of air through the levels
below and for the safety of the mine. In the
neighborhood of the last fire on this level they
have built a series of light bulkheads to pre
vent the too rapid circulation of air through
the burnt district. 1750 levelâ Of the main
wept drift 208 feet have been repaired, retlm
bered and track laid,, making In all a repaired
drift of 358 feet, and the station as remodeled
Is forty-two feet long. The repairs ln the west
drift have been carried to a point where the
bulkhead was built to control the last fire. The
electric Installation for present requirements Is
complete.
In the Ophir mine. in the old Central tunnel
workings, crosscut No. 1 was started during
the past week at a point 126 feet northeast
from the main west drift. It will make a
good crosscut of the vein and, it has been driven
fourteen feet in a mixture of quartz, clay and.
porphyry. A west drift is being cleaned out so
as to obtain a better supply of air. On the
Sutro tunnel level of the Ophlr work has con
tinued in the connection drift, which la, out
thirty-two feet ln very hard rock of the hang
ing wall. .

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrography
Office, located ln the Merchants' Exchange, 1,
maintained in San Francisco for the. ben. -fit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
"Navigators are cordially in vlted to visit thÂ»
office where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison . and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of interest to ocean commerce.
.The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street, ls hoi-ted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
en time or giving the error, If any, ls published
in the morning papers the following day.
W. S. -HUGHES,
- Lieutenant, U. S. N.. in charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early mornjng tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the last. or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the .charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of 'the* lower low waters.

dee, for Royal Roads.TEACHERS ARE
ADVISED TO
COMPROMISE
Lively Meeting Held
Yesterday.
ONLY ONE SIDE HEARD
FINAL ACTION WAS DEFERRED
UNTIL NEXT THURSDAY.
Attorney Crests-ell Urges Them Not
to Further Complicate Matters
by Refusing the Offer of the
Merchant Creditors.
About five hundred teachers met in the
auditorium of the Girls' High School yes
terday afternoon to consider the compro
mise offered by the merchant creditors of
the late School Board. They listened to
reports from the secretaries of various
committees; a long speech by Harry T.
Oreswell and other counsel, did a great
deal of talking and finally adjourned to
meet next Thursday afternoon, when final
action will be taken.
The meeting was called to order shortly i
after 4 o'clock by Elisha Brooks. He In- i
troduced C. H. Murphy and W. D. Kings- !
bury, who have been it Sacramento look- !
ing after legislation which interested the i
teachers. The two gentlemen -rave a very I
good account of themselves. Colonel :
Murphy first made an oral report, and it
was afterward supplemented by a written
one read by Mr. Kingsbury, which cover- J
ed- practically the same ground. A brief
excerpt from it tells the whole story: [
'"Every bill we tried to have passed went
through and those which did not stand
our scrutiny were defeated.*- An itemized !
list of expenditures was then read, which :
show- that it had cost the committee
$415 75 to do its work. This was so satis
factory that when Miss Emma Stincen,
who acts as treasurer, showed that a
little more money would be needed all
agreed to an assessment of 35 cents,
Messrs. Murphy and Kingsbury were
tendered a unanimous rising vote of j
Harry T. Creswell. one of the attorneys
for the teachers, then made a long state
ment. He went carefully over the ground,
covering every move made since last De
cember, explaining clearly why certain ;
actions which some considered the proper
thing-had not been taken. He said that ,
he, as one of the attorneys, had not only
endeavored to straighten out the tangle
' but had taken only such steps as would
prove most expeditious. He advised
strongly against suing the merchants to
prove the illegality of the latters' claims.
"Such a course," said he "would in
volve us in a long and expensive litiga
tion. The late school board was anything
but systematic. There are no records to
show "what was purchased and we would
be obliged to carefully question the book
keepers and managers of establishments
holding claims. The Judge would, in all
probability; appoint a referee at $10 per
cay to take evidence. Then there would
be the fees of an attorney and a stenog
rapher. Everything would have to be
taken down in full and when the notes
were transcribed we would have a pile of
evidence about three reet high. This is no
exaggeration. My advice to you. as your
attorney, is that you accept the com
promise offered by the merchants. It is
the safest course. The merchants claims
ac -~-e-*"-ie $â¢*â¢*- Â°*'"'- and there is nothing at j
it _ t-u-- to J_i-i-Â«.r them from drawing I
c"Â this =urn. There Is but $25,000 to
imeet this with, and the other $33,000 would i

If this is done the chances are that your
â -a'aries will asrain be shaved in -day or
June The merchants oner you 5-J per cent
of this sum. or $44,000. My advice U* that
vou 'accent it and avoid litigation."
- Hp was followed by Lewis Bartlett who
Wad letters from Auditor Wells. P*. A.
B^rgerot. I. J. Truman and others advls-
Ingithls course. Other speakers followed
him on the same lines, and at the conclu
sion of their remarks a motion was made
that the compromise be accepted. .he
result was chaos.
There â Â»-..-" apparently two strong iac

score people started to talk at once and

Friday It was voted do*** A teacher in
the rear of tho hall finally secured the
fioor and made a strong plea against the

ALLOTMENT LAW EVADED.
Attempts to Ship a Fifteen-Dollar
Crew on the James Drummond.
United States Shipping Commissioner
Gwin has tied up the American ship
Jam.es Drummond and the vessel will be
unable to obtain a crew until the author
ities at Washington, D. C, have been
heard from. The ship is owned in Bath,
Me-., and came to this port with a mis
cellaneous cargo. Her master, James
Skew- Intends to clear for London and
Liverpool direct, and had made all his
preparations for engaging a crew at $15
per month per man.
As the regular going rate of wages for
twenty-two consecutive months for deep
water sailors had been $20, the Commis
sioner suspected that something was
wrong and telegraphed to Washington,
D. C. yesterday for instructions. -
Only a short time ago Congress cut
down the allotment of advances on deep
water sailors to one months-p ay. Hence
It was expected that the keepers of sailor
boarding-nouses would so arrange mat
ters as to ship men ostensibly at $15 per
month and draw down the other $5 per
month by collusion with the captain.

is valued at $250. Egry got it and took it

management, the grand officers and delegates will attend in a body this even- Â£
lng, and as it is to be a Knight of Honor night the theater will be taste- 'Â£
fully decorated with banners, bunting and shields emblematic of the order. Z
At the entrance there will be a miniature forest of living trees and plants, -â¢â¢
and above all will be in letters of gold, "Welcome. Knights of Honor." 'Â£
Grand Dictator P. L. Archibald will be given the privilege of addressing a
the audience from the stage upon the order and its work, and he will talk *

MORE CONFISCATED FISH.
A Big Seizure Which Will Go to
Charitable Institutions.
The children of the Hebrew and Protest
. ant orphan asylums and the inmates of
J the Almshouse will rejoice to-day when
! their dinners are served! Such fish as
| they will eat cost wealthy men good mon
! ey, for striped bass is one of the luxuries
. of the tables of those who can afford to
have good things. For some time the fish
j ermen up the rivers have been catching
striped bass weighing less than three
I pounds, the lfmit marked by the State
j laws. As it is difficult to convict the of
! fenders the deputies of the Fish Commis
sion have decided to cause them all the
annoyance possible, and so every bass un
der weight is seized and given to those
who are dependent upon the people in
general for their support- â¢: . â¢ .
On Saturday Deputy John H. Davis
seized 1&0 pounds of these fish that ar
rived from Sobrante. and yesterday â¢_â "-")
pounds more were confiscated on the wa
ter front by the same officer. The fish run
from one and a half to three pounds, and
i in order that the lot might ecape the
! eyes of the officers a layer of flounders
j and sole was placed over the top of each
box. The Fish Commissioners are deter
â¢ mined to break up this illegal catching of
I striped bass if possible.
The Fighting Jockey.
Frederick Maynard. known as the fight-
Ing jockey, was yesterday held to answer
before the Superior Court by Judge Con
lan on a charge of assault with a deadly
weapon, in $1000 bonds. March 9, during a
fight in the Thalia, Maynard stabbed Jo
seoh Calderon, a machinist, six times
with a pocket knife, but none of the
wounds was dangerous.

ment of marriage from Mary J. "Cullen"

fWhen I see a man who is an easy and steady eater and
a sound and regular sleeper, . I see a man who has no oc-Â«
casion to be afraid of the world, the flesh or the devil. For.
from those still solemn hours of the night, when human
figures lie like recumbent statues in the darkness, and from
those other hours when busy forks and fingers deliver to
the mouth the food which the palate stands ready to wel-Â«
comeâ from these two things, I say, proceeds all human'
power, together with the clearness and sanity of mind which*,
leads it to every valuable accomplishment.
"When we sleep," said Leigh Hunt, "is the time when tha
mysterious spirit goes to take its airy rounds." "I advisa
you to sleep in peace and wake in Joy," said Sir Walter
Scott. "Sleep," said Bulwer. "is the happiest of earthly
boons." "Sleep," said a poet-philosopher of Boston, "lingers
' all our lifetime near our eyes as the night hovers all day im
What these gentle men say is very good and fine, and
makes sleep seem as easy a thing to come at as a ham
sandwich is to the fellow who has five cents still left in his
pocket. Nevertheless, if you could take the roofs off tha
houses and look down into them from above, passing from
one to another, swift as Asmodeus, you would see millions
_^ of people tumbling about on their beds, praying in vain to
j^__J the somnolent gods from early evening to the crack of day.

ALAMEDA COUNTY NEWS.
HAS ONE WIFE
MORE THAN HE
REALLY DESIRES
Dr. Cook Fails to Pay
Overdue Alimony.
. â¢
ONLY RECENTLY DIVORCED
WENT TO RENO TO BEAT THE
STATE LAW.
He Faces His First Wife in Court and
Tells of Another Wife and
Small Family, to
Support.
r * ~' Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
906 Broadway, March 20.
â  Dr. Cook, the well known physician, is
In a peck of trouble. lie has been married
to two women, and both of them expect
him to support them. Not many months
Ago Dr. Cook figured in the Oakland di
vorce courts. His wife narrated a tale of
unhappiness which impressed the Judge
as being sufficient to Justify him in grant
ing her a decree. On her showing that
Dr. Cook had a lucrative practice she was
'granted alimony. After this she lost sight
of her hut-band for a few months, and he
also forgot to pay her the alimony.
Dr. Cook was not anxious to live a
single life. It is little less than a year
since the divorce was granted, and to-day
'he was again called into court, this time
,to explain why he did not pay alimony
!to the first Mrs. Cook. The doctor then
confided to Judge Ellsworth that shortly
after his divorce he made a hurried trip
to Reno and that now ho has another
wife and family to support. And all this
has happened within a year. The first
Mrs. Cook was also in court, but there
were no loving glances cast by her at her
ex-husband when he told of how he had
'evaded the State law by crossing into
Nevada and of how- he had replaced his
first wife by her successor, who was not
in court to-day.
The first Mrs. Cook's attorney argued
that his client was entitled to support,
,but Judge Ellsworth said that he knew of
no precedent for a man being required to
.support a divorced wife when it was

PRESIDENT WM. R. HARPER. I
â¢4-
BERKELEY, March President William R. Harper of the University Â®
of Chicago, one of the most distinguished scholars of the East, is to be ~
ln California during the present' week. He comes to deliver the oration V
at the Charter day exercise, of the University of California, to bo held *Â»
in Berkeley March 23; but his stay will be fully occupied during the brief V
seven days with a veritable round of public receptions and addresses. Both q
tho pulpit and the platform will make demands upon him dally, and the rep- i
resentatlve associations of scholars and clergymen are arranging dates on Â©
which he will be their guest of honor. >
President Harper's visit is somewhat unique in that it is the first occa- V
Eion on which an Eastern college president has crossed the Rockies for the Â«
express purpose of attending the anniversary exercises of California's State h?
University, a.nd collegians are disposed to look upon the event as marking Â©
the closer union which is springing up between the universities of the East -*âº
and West. The subject of his address on Berkeley's Charter day is to be Â©
"Democracy and the University," and in it he Is expected to outline the i
most modern views of the relation between colleges and the masses. . T
President Harper will arrive In Berkeley on the morning of Wednesday, Â©
March 22. On the afternoon of the same day the student body of the unlver- â +
sity has arranged to tender him a public reception, consisting of a military Â©
parade and review on the campus, to be followed by an address in Harmon -+
gymnasium. The latter will be exclusively for members .of. the university. Â©
At half-past 10 Thursday morning the Board of Regents and the professors â +
and associate professors of the faculty will assemble in Harmon gymnasium Â©
to celebrate, with appropriate academic exorcises, the thirty-first annlver- -â¦
sary of the day on which the university first became a corporate body un- Â©
der charter from the State. Here President Harper will deliver the oration -f
which brings him to the Pacific Coast. Â©
The distinguished visitor will also spend a short time at Stanford Unlver- â¦
Elty as the guest of President David Starr Jordan, his last public address be- V
lng delivered in . Oakland next Sunday afternoon. <-_
