PROVED TO BE A FAILURE.
Married the Same Man Twice and
Now Rejects Him.
OAKLAND, March 20.â After having
twice married the same man, having been
once divorced from him, Mrs. Johanna M.
F. Schmid has finally concluded that
after all marriage is not what it is pro
claimed to be. Accordingly, she has
once more appealed to the Superior Court
to untie the nuptial knot. :; : L; - . ':..
In her complaint for a divorce from
Jacob G. Schmid, filed this afternoon,
Mrs. Schmid charges her husband with
failure to provide and extreme cruelty.
The parties were first married in 189., but
the domestic path proved too thorny, and
in March, 1896, Mrs. Schmid sued for and
secured a divorce. A few months later
the divorced couple kissed and made up
and their marriage was once more sol
emnized. The law prohibiting divorced
people from remarrying or marrying at
all within a year subsequent to a decree
was not then in effect.
The fruit of the marriage was a bright
baby girl, the custody of which the
mother now asks for. The Schmids reside
in Last Oakland.
St. Mary's Students Celebrate.
OAKLAND, March 20.â Last evening
the students of St. Mary's College cele
brated St. Patrick's day by musical and
literary exercises. The college hall was
tastefully decorated for the occasion, and
the exercises were under the auspices of
the Athletic Association of the college.
The following took part: William Eccles.
S. P. Young, O. H. La Valllere, James
Blossom, E. C. Rice, J. P. Plover. A. A.
Henno, F. W. Corcoran, E. J. Mullaly,
J. M. Lawler, W. Wetzemann, J. T. Kll
llan, F. E. Michel, J. P. Fitzgerald, W.
J. Walsh.
Realizes His Insanity.
OAKLAND, March 20.â E. Brownine.
a draughtsman, aged 32 years, was com
mitted to Napa Insane Asylum, to-day by
Judge Hall. Browning is of a very mel
ancholy disposition and at times becomes
violent. He realized that his mind is un
balanced and asked the court to commit
him to an asylum, where he might hope
for eventual recovery. He wears his
hair ln long curls, and his face is orna
mented with a unique growth of beard.
Browning states that he adopts this fasn
lon so that his appearance might be in
keeping with his condition. .
An Old Journalist Insane.
OAKLAND, March 20.â Samuel Stevens
Saul, aged 65 years, a pioneer newspaper
man, was examined for insanity to-day
by the Lunacy Commissioners and has
been recommended for committment to
an asylum.
THE SAT* FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, MARCH 21, 1899.
AN OLD-TIME
RAILROAD MAN
PASSES AWAY
Death of Thomas J.
Carothers.
OAKLAND'S BAGGAGE MASTER
'â¢MOVED TO A NEW HOME" A
MONTH AGO.
Contracted His Fatal Illness by Fi
delity to Dutyâ Proof of Deep
Devotion for His Loved
Daughter. . ;
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 20.
Thomas J. Carothers, known equally
well as "the Southern Pacific baggage
master," and an Oakland politician, died
THOMAS J. CAROTHERS.
at his residence on Harrison street to
day. For twenty-seven years Mr. Car
others has been actively engaged ln busi
ness in this city. In 1872 he came to this
city from Sacramento and entered the
employ of the railroad company. He has
never changed his employment, and was
well known by the thousands of people
who pass through the Southern Pacific
depot.
Soon after his arrival Mr. Carothers
took a prominent part in the politics of
Oakland. He was elected to the City
Council from the First Ward several
years ago, and gave such satisfaction to
his constituents that they returned him
a second and third time. When M. C.
Chapman was Mayor he appointed Mr.
Carothers a member of the Board of Pub
lic Works. At that time the board ex
ercised more power than any other
branch of the city government, having
full power to distribute all the patronage
In the Police. Fire and Street depart-,
ments.
The deceased affiliated with several fra
ternal societies, being a member of Har
bor Lodge, 1. O. O. F. ; Occidental Lodge,
A. O. U. W., and Golden Gate Lodge,
Knights of Honor, in each of which he
held Important positions.
Mr. Carothers attributed the origin of
his fatal illness to the amount of work he
was called upon to perform at the Na
tional Christian Endeavor Convention.
The blockade of travel and baggage on
that occasion fell heavily on his depart
ment, and he was required to go for three
days without a minute's rest. This pro
duced complications which ultimately re
sulted in death. Sixty-two years ago Mr.
Carothers was born at Washington, D.
C. He came I . California with his broth
er in 1855, both having received appoint
ments as mall agents for the Government.
After arriving in this State the brothers
resigned their Government appointments
and went into business in Sacramento
upon a large scale. They acquired valua
ble property just outside the capital city,
which they still own.
The funeral will take place Wednesday
afternoon. During his lifetime Mr. Car
others frequently requested that there be
no religous services at the house, and that
the ceremonies be as simple as possible.
His wishes will be carried out, even to
the extent of not. permitting friends to
send the customary floral tributes. The
deceased leaves a widow, but their only
child died several years ago.
A few weeks ago Mr. Carothers moved
Into his new home. There is one room in
it that was of special interest to him. It
was the room that would have been occu
pied by his only daughter. Lillian, had she
lived. The plans were made before her
death and "-"** parents declined to alter
them. The girl's room was furnished just
as though she were living, and such fur
niture, bric-a-brac and other things as she
had prized were retained as a memorial
of an only child.
Mrs. Baven's Strange Death.
OAKLAND, March 20.â Dr. R. T. Strat
ton will conduct an autopsy to-morrow on
the body of Mrs. Iva Raven, who died at
the Receiving Hospital to-day under
rather peculiar, circumstances. Mrs. Ra
ven was a domestic in the employ of R
M. Clement, . City Engineer-elect, and
yesterday morning she complained of
pains In her stomach. She was taken to
the Receiving Hospital and shortly after
arriving there became unconscious and
remained in that state until the end,
which came this morniing.
The physicians at the hospital were un
able to ascertain the nature of the
woman's illness or the cause of death
Coroner Mehrmann will also hold an In
quest to-morrow night. Mrs. Raven was
with her husband last Saturday night
and he has been subpenaed to attend me
Inquest. Deceased was a native of Nor
way, aged 41 years.

LOWER WATER
RATES FIXED
FOR OAKLAND
Council Passes the
New Ordinance.
WAKEFIELD MAY BE UNSEATED
WAS NOT NATURALIZED IN
TIME TO HOLD OFFICE.
In Conflict With the Provisions of
the City Canvassing
the Returns of the Re
cent Election.
OAKLAND, March 20.â The City Coun
cil passed the Upton water-rate ordinance
to-night with only two dissenting votes.
This ends the water-rate business for this
year. The schedule, which will go into
effect July 1, places the rates about mid
way between those passed last year and
the Manuel ordinance passed four years
ago. It was based on the investigation
made by the. minority of the Council and
is slightly higher than the ordinance pre
sented by Councilman Earl, who Is a
member of the Fire and Water Commit
tee. The only votes against it were those
of Earl and Brosnahan. Not for several
years has the water rate matter been dis
posed of so smoothly.
The Council commenced to-night to
canvass the returns of the late election.
When the official returns are made it is
possible that Dr. W. F. B. Wakefield will
not be permitted to take his seat. The
great register shows that W. F. B. Wake
field, a native of Canada, was on July 30,
i*-:<-.. naturalized, and that on the same
day he was registered. The city charter
says, "The Mayor, members of the Coun
cil, Auditor, Treasurer, City Attorney,
Police Judge, Commissioners of Public
Works, School Directors, members of the
Board of Health and Health Officer must
each at the time of the election or ap
pointment have been a citizen of the
United States and a resident and qualified
elector of the city for three years next
preceding their election or appointment."
The Board of Education will fill the va
cancy when It occurs, but not until the
canvass is complete can any action be
taken. i__3&
The canvass proceeded till a third of the
returns were gone over. All the leading
candidates had representatives , present,
but there were no changes from the un
official returns already published. The
canvass will be resumed at 7:30 o'clock
Tuesday night. '
ROETH'S HIGH WATER
RATE IS ADOPTED
MITCHELL'S OBDINANCE BE
JECTED.
Supervisors Fix a Schedule for Out
side Consumers for the Next
Fiscal Year.
.Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, March 20.
The water rates for all of this county
outside of incorporated towns were fixed
by the Supervisors to-day. For many
weeks the matter has been under discus
sion and this morning two ordinances
were before the board. One was intro
duced by Supervisor Roeth; the other by
Supervisor Mitchell. Roeth's ordinance
is about, 25 per cent higher than Mit
chell's. For the smallest of houses Roeth
would charge 45 cents, and Mitchell 25
cents, and this graded until a large four
story house Is. reached, when Mitchell
would charge $2 60 and Roeth $3 15.
The rates for extras, such as lawns, ani
mals and other uses to which water ls
put, are in the same proportion. When
the ordinances were read Mr. Roeth
stated that his figures were based upon
the rates now In use, with the exception'
of an increase of 25 per cent for water for
sprinkling purposes.
Mr. Mitchell said he thought existing
rates were too high, and defended his or
dinance on the ground that his rates were
just and equitable. Mitchell's ordinance
was voted on first and was defeated, the
vote standing: For the ordinance, Mit
chell and Talcott; against. Church and
Roeth, while Wells was excused.
The Roeth ordinance was then taken' up
and passed by the votes of Church, Roeth
and Wells. Mitchell and Talcott voted
against it. The new rates go into effect
i July 1.

to-day set the third trial of James ' F.
O'Brien Jr., charged with felony, for
May 2. The trial will probably prove

vestigating.

BLACKSTOCK GIVES UP
TO SOUTHERN PACIFIC
Advises Abandonment of All Liti
gation and All Efforts to Se
cure Rate Reductions.
â 'â .'â 'â¢' . â *
Bis Colleagues Decline to Act on His Resolution and
Ask for Time to Consider Bis Extraordinary
Proposition.
I am unqualifiedly of the opinion that the continuance of this
litigation is not in the interest of the people of the State, and that
it is the duty of this board, so far as it has the power, to terminate
this litigation, so that it may free its hands and save further ex
pense to the State. ... I find it to be in the power of this
commission, without the consent of the railroad company, to ter
minate this litigation by repeal and rescindment of the whole resolu
tion, covering the 8 per cent grain rate reduction as well as the
25 per cent general horizontal reduction.-- Excerpt from report
of Railroad Commissioner Blackstock.
IT was in the above quoted words that
Railroad Commissioner Blackstock, at
the meeting of the Railroad Commis
sion, yesterday, proposed to surrender
to the ' Southern Pacific Company the
fruits of three years of expensive litiga
tion undertaken in behalf of the farmers,
merchants and shippers of this State.
This language was part of the report he
submitted to his colleagues, and Its sub
stance was subsequently embodied in a
formal resolution, action on which was
deferred until to-day.
If this resolution is adopted it will give
to the railroad corporation even more
than it had the audacity to ask in its
overtures for a compromise with the pre
decessors of the present commission.
H. M. La Rue, the chairman of the for
mer board, stated, and his assertion has
not been contradicted, that an offer was
made to him on behalf of the Southern
Pacific by W. H. Mills to settle the pend
ing controversy on the following terms:
Tho Southern Pacific Company would put
into effect the 8 per cent reduction on
grain rates, and would dismiss its suit
restraining the action of the Railroad
Commission, if the latter would agree to
forego any action on the 25 per cent gen
eral horizontal reduction resolution.
It is claimed that the railroad made this
proposition in order to escape the ordeal i
of having its books and papers brought
into court and its affairs and business
methods thrown open to the public, as
would be the case If the litigation were
pressed to a conclusion in the courts.
Those familiar with the situation hold
that such a ventilation arid investigation
would probably result in demonstrating
that the commission would be justified in
making radical rate reductions all along
the line, and that it is this danger which
the Southern Pacific has been desperately
seeking to avoid for the past three years.
By the resolution of Commissioner
Blackstock the terms of this proposed
compromise are not only accepted but the
corporation is exempted from the effects j
of the 8 per cent reduction in grain rates
throughout the State.
The reason assigned by Commissioner
Blackstock for his extraordinary resolu
tion is that to continue the litigation
would be a mtter of great expense to the
â¢ people, while the saving to the grain
shippers would not exceed $30,000 a year.
Commissioner Blackstock had been ap
pointed a committee of one to investigate
the pending litigation regarding the 8 per
cent reduction of grain rates and the pro
posed 25 per cent reduction * of general
freight rates ordered by the last Board.
lie submitted his report yesterday. After
a complete review of the present status
of the case he presented his resolution
providing for the abandonment of the liti
gation and requested that immediate ac
tion be taken upon it.
Commissioner Laumeister, while ex
pressing himself in favor of the resclnd
ment of the 25 per cent general horizontal
reduction, was opposed to the repeal of
the resolution passed by ' the previous
board inasmuch as it related to the 8 per
cent reduction on grain rates. He asked
for a postponement of its consideration.
Commissioner Edson expressed himself
In a similar strain, and thought that time
should be allowed himself and his col
league to consider the report of Mr.
Blackstock In. all Its essential points. The
latter rather unwillingly agreed to * the
postponement, and his argument in favor
of his recommendation will be heard this
afternoon.
The report in part is as follows:
So far as this case is concerned, it appears
that the only material question presented ls as
to whether or not the 8 per cent grain rate
schedule, promulgated by the commission is
fair, Jurt and reasonable. It Is a matter of
common knowledge, and undoubtedly the* fact.

Thousands of women fail of this for one
great prevalent reason. While they resort
to every device to preserve their beauty of
complexion by external applications and
possibly take the best care of their general
health, they are too frequently utterly neg-
lectful of their health in a special womanly
way. No woman can remain beautiful, at-
tractive or amiable, who suffers from weak-
ness and disease of the delicate and import
ant feminine organism. A woman who
suffers in this way will lose her clearness
of complexion, her eyes will become dull,
worry and pain will mark her face with
wrinkles and she will become nervous,
sickly, fretful and despondent. There is a
sovereign remedy for all troubles of this
description. It is Dr. Pierces Favorite
Prescription. ;> *
" Mv illness was causr/d by lack of medical at-
tention d'uriug childbirth. It lasted for a period
of three years, during which my suffering was
almost indescribable,'' writes Mrs. Edith Petty,
of Tcxanna, Cherokee Nat., Ind. Ter. " Owing
to the injuries receivedâ rupture, internal dis-
placement, etc.. I became a physical wreck. I
was in a constant state of pain and nervous col-
lapse. I became so nervous. I feared insanity.
My heart was affected and I had spells of palpi-
tation and smothering. Was reduced in strength
and could take no solid food. The doctors told
me that recovery was impossible without a sur-
gical operation. * My monthly periods ceased en-
tirely. My mother bought a bottle of Dr. Pierces
Favorite Prescription. I took twelve bottles.
Thanks to an All-wise Providence and Doctor
Pierces medicines, the symptoms have all dis-
appeared. I can now do a hard day's work, eat
anything and everything I wish. I regard my
recovery as permanent, for it is nearly two years
aiacc I stopped taking Dr. Pierces medicines."
that the Southern Pad flo Company, In order

road company and the rates proposed by the
S per cent grain rate schedule Is very small,
such difference, in all probability, not affect
ing the revenue of the company to an extent
exceeding $30,000 per annum. Certainly, as the
case now stands, the substantial pecuniary
matters In Issue are slight, and the question is
whether a decision of these matters in favor
of the State will Justify the great expense and
delay which must be incurred in order to reach
a final decision. â¢ â¢
I am advised by the Attorney General, and by
counsel retained the Attorney by our prede
sel retained ln this case by our prede
cessors, that it will require enormous labor and
a very wide range of Investigation of expert
witnesses and the expenditure of not less than
$30,000 to prepare the case for submission by
the respondents to the Circuit Court for de
cision; that it will take years to 'try Itâ
counsel stating that It will require possibly
five or six years, another one that it will take
not less than four years to take the case
through the Supreme Court of the United
States, where It would Inevitably be taken
should it be pushed to final Judgment. I am
further advised, as appears by the records and
by the report of the Attorney General to the
Assembly, that up to the present time there
has been paid and incurred in counsel fees and
expenses in this case more than $43,000, the
counsel fees alone amounting to more than
$33,000, and It further appears from like sources
that the future costs and expenses of this liti
gation will be at least $67.000. $30,000 of which
will bo required as fees for experts and $30,000
as fees for special counsel. Should we press the
case with diligence, in my own opinion It would
require at least the remainder of the terms of
office of the present commission to bring It to
a final conclusion, and during all this time the
hands of the commission would be bo tied that
it -practically could not investigate or make
any reduction or changes in the rates now en
forced. It seems to me that, even If we could
be assured of a final judgment ln this case,
the expenditure of time and money necessary
to accomplish this end would not be Justified,
even to reach such a result; on the contrary,
it seems clear that the people are Interested
far more In securing actual reductions of rates
whenever the same may be' had, rather than
that they should bear the heavy burdens of this
litigation, even to secure a. favorable decision,
which, as I have shown, would accomplish very
little for the actual benefit of the people.
"While not desiring to criticize the acts of our
predecessors, It is evident that a serious mis
take was made in adopting the so-called 25
per cent freight rate resolution. Had the com
mission confined its official action to the pro
mulgation of rates actually determined upon. It
is not likely that any litigation would have
resulted, ln which case the people would have
secured at once the benefits of the reductions
made and would not have been- called upon to
bear the expenses of this litigation, the out
come of which ls exceedingly uncertain at best.
It 16 the duty of the commission to secure, so
far as It can legally, reductions In rates of
transportation, and It ls evident that no such
result can be accomplished by any general hor
izontal reduction of the rates maintained by
the company: on the contrary,- the only practi
cal method of securing any reduction Is to In
vestigate each class of rates and to remove
such discriminations as may be found, and to
reduce apy particular rates or classes of rates
which may be found exorbitant to the basis
of a reasonable and fair rate. ' When this ls
done It Is certain that' the company will not
have . the advantages which it has secured In
the present litigation, growing out of the par
ticular form of the resolution adopted by the
board.
In view of these undoubted conditions exist
ing at this date and of the results which are
certain to accrue in the future, is It our - duty,
representing the State and exercising sound
discretion as the agents of the State, to make
the extraordinary expenditure, without com
pensatory results, which undoubtedly will be
required ln the future prosecution of this case?
I am unqualifiedly of the opinion that the con
tinuance of this litigation is. not in the Interest
of the people of the State, and that is the
duty of this board, so far as It has the power,
to terminate this litigation so that It may free
its hands and save further expense to the State
and thereafter proceed by regular methods to
secure such advantages as may be had for the
benefit of the shippers of freight.
' I find It to be in the power of this com
mission, without the consent of the railroad
company, to terminate this litigation by re
peal, and rescindment of the whole resolution
covering the 8 per cent grain rate, reduction as
well as the 25 per cent- general horizontal re
duction. By the rescindment and repeal of this
resolution the entire basis of the suit is re
moved and the case must fall, and with it the
Injunction against this commission, thus leav
ing the commission free to adopt rates based
upon present conditions, and to adjust. Is pos
sible, the â¢ numerous complaints constantly
coming before the board 'of discriminations in
freight and passenger charges. This, to my
mind, manifestly is the only proper course to
pursue, so that the hands of the commission
may be untied and It can for itself take the
responsibility of putting Into effect the con
stitutional and statutory' provisions of the State
as Judge McKerina has most clearly defined
them to exist.
In pursuance of the conclusions reached by
me and stated in the foregoing report, I have

THE MONITOR IS NOW
EDITED BY A LAYMAN
TJ.O.MJIS m COÂ«NJ.ELLY of the "Cleveland Universe " Ap
pointed by -Hpefybishop Riopdan.
THE Monitor will in the future be edited- by a layman instead -of by a
priest, as It has been In the. past. Mr. Thomas" A. Connelly, . formerly
editor of the Cleveland 'Universe of Cleveland, Ohio, has been I appointed
to the position by Archbishop Riordan and arrived from the East a few'
days ago and yesterday took charge of his new office. -<<"'-
Ever since the Rev. Father P. C. Yorke left for Europe , the paper has
been taken care of and edited by -several of the Catholic clergy specially ap
pointed by his Grace, but they found that the duties were..very trying on
them, and at th.eir request a layman was appointed. 'â â¢..Â»â¢ Â
Thomas A. Connelly is a young man, yet he Is old In journalistic; training.
Previous to his seven j years' experience as editor of the Cleveland Universe
he was attached to the staff of Such papers as the New York Star, the
Washington Capital,' under- Don Piatt, the New York ! Commercial Adver
tiser, and did five years' effective work on the Baltimore Mirror.'-Mr. Connelly's
labors on the Cleveland Universe won the admiration of his confreres of the
Catholic press of the country. By his brilliant pen and tireless industry he
placed the Universe among the very best of the Catholic papers'.
Though the Monitor will in the future be edited by a layman. Archbishop
Riordan will still take a lively Interest in the paper and will no doubt offer
suggestions as to' the paper's policy. When seen yesterday the new editor
stated that he had as yet not decided to make any changes in the staff and
that he would not do so until he had consulted with the Archbishop. This is
Mr. Connelly's first visit to the coast. ' His wife and family will join him
here as soon as he can make the necessary arrangements.

111 health has forced Elvia Crox Sea
brooke, the popular soubrette of the Tiv
oli company, to retire for an indefinite

quaintances and the regular patrons of

will spend the next few months in the
dry, arid zone of Arizona and New Mex
ico. ,*"i ~.i-
Mlss Crox has been suffering for some
time with pulmonary trouble which grad
ually increased until her physician
warned her that she must cease signing
for a time or her voice would be irre
trievably lost. Reluctantly she accepted
the doctor's advice and last Sunday even
ing made her last appearance for some
time at the Tivoli. It is hoped that two
or three months' rest will entirely restore
her health. The moist climate of San
Francisco has been very trying on tho
young lady and it Is possible that she will
not return to this city. The manage
ment of the Tivoli expresses regret that
their favorite has been compelled to re
tire.

MRS. DR. MUFFE
ENDEAVORS TO
KILL HERSELF
Grief Leads to an At
tempt at Suicide. .
POOR AIM SAVED HER LIFE
INCONSOLABLE OVER RECENT
DEATH OF HER HUSBAND.
Her Great Loss Brought on Hysteria
and Melancholia Friends Hope '
She Will Soon Recover
Mental Balance.
While suffering from hysteria, the re
sult of poignant grief over the recent
death of her husband, Mrs. Dr. Frederick
P. Muffe made what was happily an un
successful attempt at suicide yesterday.
Ever since the sudden and untimely de
mise of Dr. Muffe his widow has been
half-crazed with sorrow, and as the days
go by she seems to feel her loss more
keenly. Her daughters have done every
thing in the power of loving children to
console their heart-broken mother, being
deeply concerned for her physical and
mental health, but until, yesterday they
had no suspicion that she ever thought
of ending her sufferings by suicide.
Late yesterday afternoon the report of
a pistol startled the inmates of the Muffe.
home at 1211 Jackson street, and as it
came from the room occupied by the be
reaved widow it created consternation and
gave her daughter Agnes a horrifying
shock. Mrs. Muffe had secured -possession
of a pistol and vainly attempted to shoot
herself, but in her nervous, hysterical con
dition she pulled tho trigger before the
muzzle was pointed in her direction and
the bullet was imbedded in the wall. She
did not attempt to lire a second shot.
Kindly hands led her to her chamber and
she was lovingly soothed and persuaded
to retire. A doctor was called and pre
scribed a sleeping potion, and last even
ing the patient was resting quietly.
Mrs. Muffe- nervous system Is com
pletely shattered, yet her family hope her
recovery will be speedy and comDlete.
The numerous friends of the late doctor,
who is remembered for his many acts of
kindness, join with the family in their
sorrow and express thankfulness that his
.widow escaped the bullet fired by her own
hand while she was grief-crazed.
'Dr. Muffe died on the morning of March
10 of heart disease. He was seized with a
paroxysm, and while his wife was prepar
ing a remedy to relieve his agony he ex
pired. The shock, followed by the sad in
cidents of the funeral; was too great, and
the unhappy widow has been suffering
â from melancholia and despondency ever
since. Her two daughters are Mrs. H.
Baum of Fort Bragg and Miss Agnes Jes
sie Muffe. â 
TWO BANKS WILL
RESUME BUSINESSS
APPLICATIONS MADE TO THE
BANK COMMISSION.
Assets of the Institutions Greatly
Enhanced in Value by the
Southern Rains.
.Two of the four banks that suspended
payment in San Uuis Obispo County last
week have felt- the result of the recent
copious and opportune rains. Communi
cations were received by the Bank Com
mission yesterday from the officers of the
Commercial Bank of San Luis Obispo and
of the Citizens' Bank of Paso Robles,
stating that owing to the enhancement in
value of the real estate held by them,
caused by the storm, they were prepare
to resume business.
In the case of the Commercial Bank the
commission was informed that the stock
holders were ready to contribute an ad
ditional $50,000 to the capital of the insti
tution and that most of the depositors
had agreed not to make withdrawals for
certain stipulated peri ds, varying from
three to nine months.
The stockholders of the Citizens' Bank
have arranged to pay in .$15,000 cash and
the depositors have made concessions
which will prevent, anything like a run
on the bank. .
There will be a meeting of all the mem
bers of the commission to-day to act on
the applications of the institutions to re
sume business, and it is believed that
they will be permitted to do so.

UNITARIAN CLUB
DISCUSSES MANY
PUBLIC TOPICS
The Great Future of
the Pacific.
BRIGHT BUSINESS OUTLOOK
SIGNIFICANCE TO CALIFORNIA
OF IMPORTANT EVENTS.
Able Speakers Discuss the Prospects
of the Countries and Islands
of the Pacific From States
men's Standpoint.
â 
Enjoyable social Intercourse and discus
sion of topics of public importance were
in order hist evening at the meeting or
the Unitarian Club of California winch
was held in the rooms of the Merchants
Club. About 100 members of the club and
guests were in attendance. They assem
bled at 6 o'clock and spent over an hour
in discussing dinner. At the close of the
feast the club listened to a discussion or
the significant events that have recently
been crowding upon one another in the
Pacific Ocean, by speakers capable of dis
cussing them. The trend of the speeches
showed the paramount importance to
California of the changes that have oc
curred. President Sheldon G. Kellogg
presided over the meeting and introduced
the speakers. 'â¢-;'-â  J _Â» .. ,
Eli T. Sheppard, for many years united
States Consul in China, was the first
speaker. His subject was the recent de
velopments In China and Russian prog
ress in the Orient. â¢ His remarks were
concise and to the point. He referred to
the fact that -as a result of the recent
war America is of necessity materially in
terested in the trade of the Pacific Ocean,
and in consequence this means a great fu
ture for the Pacific Coast and San _ ran
cisco in particular. Speaking of the na
tional influences now at work, which are
slowly dismembering China. Mr. Sheppard
said that the probable effect upon the
trade of America is a question that re
quires the most careful and matured con
sideration. China, he said, was now In
a vortex of international struggles.
France and Russia represent the policy
of territorial acquisition by spoilization,
while England and America stand for the
integrity of China. Of all the. agencies
at work in Asia there are none equal to
Russia. Her designs and policy cannot
be comprehended,* because they have
never been frankly avowed. Russia
love of conquest is solely her excuse for
such gigantic Asiatic dominion as she
now possesses. The results of the Span
ish War, which gives to this country the
Philippines, necessitate that America
shall have some definite polio* in China.
In the future, Mr. Sheppard declared, our
policy must be a firm one, as it is the
duty of this country to protect her own
trade interests in China and not trust to
England to do so, as has been the case.
The speaker said in his opinion America
and England must combine to protect
their natural rights. He showed. the
many advantages that would result as a
consequence of this co-operation.
C Count liirokichi Mutsu, the Japanese
Consul was next introduced by President
Kellogg. He dwelt on the future of the
Pacific and in substance said that one of
the most interesting subjects was a Study
of the trade between America and Japan
The latter country is the second greatest
buyer of American exports. Of all the im
ports at least one-fourth come from
Sa pan. which fact shows the close trade
relation of the two countries. After the
war with China. Japan's energies were
turned toward development and expan
sion and various business enterprises
sprang up all over the empire. The ag
gressive future of the empire is shown by
the Increase in the Japanese navy. The
empire of the rising sun is awake to the
situation in the Pacific., it realizes
that the future of the Philippines and the
Hawaiian group must go hand in- hand.
He predicted continued pleasant re
lations between Japan and America. â 
Professor Bernard. Moses of the Uni
versity of California treated the future of
the Pacific Ocean from the standpoint of
a historian. One of his remarks was most
significant and striking. He said as the
American nation had advanced from the
east to the west it. had met new condi
tions and achieved successful results on
new soil. The nation to-day is as full of
aggressive expansion as it ever was. If
the nation increases in the future as it
has done in the past it must of necessity
overrun the boundary of the United
States, and that the outlook is toward the
islands In and beyond the Pacific. Pro
fessor Moses clearly showed by concise
logic that all of the new questions arising
for solution at the hands of this Govern
ment are capable of solution, and that
what America immediately needs and
must have is diplomats, who must pos
sess business as well as -diplomatic knowl
edge. The many questions of climate,
labor and government in our new posses
sions are capable of solution, and will not
be hard to adjust. Professor Moses was
loudly' applauded at the conclusion "of his
remarks. -:_* . _. . * â,_.,,
Hon. W. R. Castle of Honolulu followed.
His remarks were entirely relative to the
islands, of which he is a native. He said
Hawaii was fitted to take its place as the
part- of a great civilized nation. To.-day
the islands, declared the speaker, are as
much American soil as any one of the
States of this Union. He showed that
financially, socially and otherwise Hawaii
was worthy to take place as a part of the
United States. " :- *
Captain Henry Glass, formerly of the
Charleston, also made a few remarks on
his personal observations of the islands.
